<div id="review_1044847990" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1044847990" />
    <a title="Suzanne" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3695758-suzanne"><img alt="Suzanne" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1369500256p2/3695758.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1044847990?book_show_action=true">Sep 03, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Suzanne" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Suzanne" href="/user/show/3695758-suzanne">Suzanne</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780385043991" href="/book/show/822843.Giles_Goat_Boy">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3695758-suzanne?shelf=metafictional-madness-and-pomo-fun">metafictional-madness-and-pomo-fun</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1044847990" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4965478026500972286">Finished this book 4 weeks ago, but better late than never. <br/><br/>I’d thought I’d read <i>Giles Goat-Boy</i> a few years ago and didn’t remember much of it because I just wasn’t enamored, despite my lifelong devotion to John Barth. But when I got to page 100, I knew I was in virgin territory and had never seen the subsequent pages before. Apparently I had put this aside on that other attempt. And then I began to realize why. <br/><br/>Because it’s a 700– page comic allegory, that’s why! This is something you really ne</span>
  <span id="freeText4965478026500972286" style="display:none">Finished this book 4 weeks ago, but better late than never. <br /><br />I’d thought I’d read <i>Giles Goat-Boy</i> a few years ago and didn’t remember much of it because I just wasn’t enamored, despite my lifelong devotion to John Barth. But when I got to page 100, I knew I was in virgin territory and had never seen the subsequent pages before. Apparently I had put this aside on that other attempt. And then I began to realize why. <br /><br />Because it’s a 700– page comic allegory, that’s why! This is something you really need to be in the mood for, and I just wasn’t either time. There are some standard Barthian elements that I enjoyed, such as his playful language and its particular rhythms that just synch up with something in my neural circuitry. I do love his prose style as much as that of any writer on earth. And I cannot help but be amazed by his general brilliance that is capable of producing something as metafictionally and allegorically complex as this, even if its wackiness was over the top and then some. And there is a parody of Oedipus Rex that’s a hoot! No, really. It was funny stuff. <br /><br />I understand that <i>GGB</i> was the only one of John Barth’s novels to ever achieve any commercial success, even though it is said to be his least favorite of his own books. I can see how college students in the mid 1960s could have gotten off on this, especially if there were earnest late night political and philosophical discussions under the influence of funny cigarettes. But the cartoonishness of it undermined any engagement I might have felt and ultimately left me pretty cold. <br /><br />Some complain about the racism and sexism in this book and that would be with good reason, if any of this could be taken seriously, but again, it’s all so abstract and allegorical, and the characters, well, so silly, I found it hard to be offended.<br /><br />I’m making it a long-term project to read or re-read the first six Barth novels in preparation for <i>Letters</i>, which will be a new one for me and which, I understand, relies on knowledge of the first six. It’s been a long time since I’ve read some of the early ones and <i>Lost in the Funhouse</i> will be for the first time. Even though <i>Giles Goat-Boy</i> was a bit of a disappointment, I’m moving on to a re-read of <i>The Sot- Weed Factor</i> sometime this winter or next spring, which has never disappointed. It’s one of my favorite books of all time, and never fails to comfort, delight, amaze, and amuse.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="4965478026500972286" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1115460" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1115460" />
    <a title="Aaron" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/80727-aaron"><img alt="Aaron" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/m_50x66-82093808bca726cb3249a493fbd3bd0f.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1115460?book_show_action=true">May 09, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Aaron" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Aaron" href="/user/show/80727-aaron">Aaron</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Pursuers of intellectualism</span></div>

        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1115460" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1999526443802240443">Giles Goat Boy, as with most of Barth's writing, cannot be summated by anything short of a novel in itself. It is a farce on heroic tales, riddled with metaphors and allusions as thick as the diction therein, with enough mass to leave a reader spending hours picking apart each sentence. This is not a bad thing by any means, as the work can be read fluidly first - then meticulously, to fully benefit from Barth's genius. <br/><br/>The Cold War, Homeric Epics, Religion, Sex, and the pretentious atmosphere of</span>
  <span id="freeText1999526443802240443" style="display:none">Giles Goat Boy, as with most of Barth's writing, cannot be summated by anything short of a novel in itself. It is a farce on heroic tales, riddled with metaphors and allusions as thick as the diction therein, with enough mass to leave a reader spending hours picking apart each sentence. This is not a bad thing by any means, as the work can be read fluidly first - then meticulously, to fully benefit from Barth's genius. <br /><br />The Cold War, Homeric Epics, Religion, Sex, and the pretentious atmosphere of Academia are among the many tools Barth utilizes to allegorize the ridiculousness of society as well as one's place in the world. Hysterical and timeless, Giles Goat-Boy tackles so many issues with such finesse, Barth makes postmodernism actually seem appealing. </span>
  <a data-text-id="1999526443802240443" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_39786969" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/39786969" />
    <a title="Josiah Miller" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1097751-josiah-miller"><img alt="Josiah Miller" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1245033039p2/1097751.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer39786969" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6184084722777995791">To start off, I don't feel this book is long-winded by any means; It's just winded enough with all the flatulence. To start off the novel with the "Editors'" reluctance to publish, rejection of manuscript and another's remonstrating of the other editors' opinions takes some big goat balls and a certain amount of ego to shape this novel into the narcissistic bundle of joy that had ever been authored by a computer. Some 20 years later Allan Bloom wrote "The Closing of the American Mind" in which h</span>
  <span id="freeText6184084722777995791" style="display:none">To start off, I don't feel this book is long-winded by any means; It's just winded enough with all the flatulence. To start off the novel with the "Editors'" reluctance to publish, rejection of manuscript and another's remonstrating of the other editors' opinions takes some big goat balls and a certain amount of ego to shape this novel into the narcissistic bundle of joy that had ever been authored by a computer. Some 20 years later Allan Bloom wrote "The Closing of the American Mind" in which he outlines a model University life and Education that New Tammany College seems to produce; a conservative, racist, hierarchical and Platonic educational system that is plagued by the nature of sexuality. With command of the English language and a joke in every sentence, John Barth has written a novel that encompasses the absurdity and debauchery that I've only been able to find and relate to in a hand full of literature.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6184084722777995791" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<span id="freeTextContainer10314761187511609225">If this book has one problem, it's that it's dated and it shows. To really appreciate this book, you have to put yourself in the mindset of the early 60's when the Cold War was gaining more and more force, beatniks were still relevant and rape was no big thing (or at least a possible laughing matter), and even blacks could be made fun of as well as Christianity. Putting it all into the perspective of a university campus which represents the world, and adding a parallel with Oedipus Rex was brill</span>
  <span id="freeText10314761187511609225" style="display:none">If this book has one problem, it's that it's dated and it shows. To really appreciate this book, you have to put yourself in the mindset of the early 60's when the Cold War was gaining more and more force, beatniks were still relevant and rape was no big thing (or at least a possible laughing matter), and even blacks could be made fun of as well as Christianity. Putting it all into the perspective of a university campus which represents the world, and adding a parallel with Oedipus Rex was brilliantly done but this book suffered from too much ambition and a joke carried too far. "The Sotweed Factor" was MUCH better.</span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1665914482" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1665914482" />
    <a title="David" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/30564033-david"><img alt="David" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/m_50x66-82093808bca726cb3249a493fbd3bd0f.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1665914482?book_show_action=true">Mar 02, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="David" class="user" itemprop="url" name="David" href="/user/show/30564033-david">David</a>
      </span>

          added it


          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780385043991" href="/book/show/822843.Giles_Goat_Boy">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1665914482" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11556143178562713998">Unlike this novel’s immediate predecessor, The Sot-Weed Factor, I found Giles Goat-Boy rather less effective on re-reading. It seems to me almost a one-joke book, a single trick spread over 700+ pages—perhaps even a bit sophomoric (and what a loaded word that one is in the context of this novel‘s use of the university as metaphor). However, I did find much of it still engaging. Perhaps the fittest epigraph to John Barth’s entire career, at the very least the first two decades or so of it, might </span>
  <span id="freeText11556143178562713998" style="display:none">Unlike this novel’s immediate predecessor, The Sot-Weed Factor, I found Giles Goat-Boy rather less effective on re-reading. It seems to me almost a one-joke book, a single trick spread over 700+ pages—perhaps even a bit sophomoric (and what a loaded word that one is in the context of this novel‘s use of the university as metaphor). However, I did find much of it still engaging. Perhaps the fittest epigraph to John Barth’s entire career, at the very least the first two decades or so of it, might be drawn from the Scottish play: “function / Is smother’d in surmise, and nothing is / But what is not.” Such is so much the case in Giles Goat-Boy that I began to expect Harold Bray to be revealed at any moment as actually being Henry Burlingame (v. the aforementioned Sot-Weed Factor, and contemplate the initials!). I was not surprised to see direct illusion to some of Barth’s earlier work; for example, a character who is qualified as a nurse is said at one point to know what to do until the doctor comes—a direct reference to Barth’s second novel The End of the Road. Still less was I surprised to see Funhouse references given that Lost in the Funhouse, the next Barth publication, must have been at least in the draft stages as this novel drew towards publication. The two texts even share elements such as the supposed transcription of recorded/taped voice to print. But when I encountered an image that appears directly 20 years later in The Tidewater Tales, I was a bit more surprised. One wonders how long Barth has held onto some of these ideas. But, of course, a quick look at any writer’s notebook—say Anthony Powell’s A Writer’s Notebook for example—reveals that some ideas enter a writer’s consciousness many decades before they enter the writer’s oeuvre. The philosophy of the book, it is much as it can be at all effectively summarized, seems to be but the idea of setting things in opposition—pass/fail, good/bad, male/female etc.—is generally a restriction on human development and that we should, as much as possible, embrace, reconcile, even celebrate our contradictions (perhaps Walt Whitman has some influence here: “Do I contradict myself! Very well, I contradict myself. I am large. I contain multitudes”). All systems of belief—spiritual, political, social—have much more in common than most such systems like to admit, and much of the energy devoted to defining ourselves by that which we are not, or that which is in opposition to us, is wasted energy, manipulated by such systems, and devoted largely to distracting us while keeping us “in place.” George Giles, the titular goat-boy, at one point refers to “inversions-of-inversions” in our logic (logical?) attempts at self-comprehension, then later observes “No matter that accessory features of the denouement were changed; it was the same old plot.” It may serve as a fair summary of both the strengths and weaknesses of the novel.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11556143178562713998" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_144642158" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/144642158" />
    <a title="C. Quabela" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2606780-c-quabela"><img alt="C. Quabela" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1540861330p2/2606780.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/144642158?book_show_action=true">Jan 30, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="C. Quabela" class="user" itemprop="url" name="C. Quabela" href="/user/show/2606780-c-quabela">C. Quabela</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2606780-c-quabela?shelf=my-library">my-library</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2606780-c-quabela?shelf=literature">literature</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer144642158" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2813236601521182663">What can one possible say of this novel? It is by far one of the most interesting pieces of American Literature of its time. One would want to consider it as science fiction or fantasy, all the while never feeling quite satisfied with either distinction (distinctions which are in themselves scrutinized in the story and possibly its most earnest (though disinterested) message). One thing for sure though is that with this novel Barth breaches that point of no return in meta-fictional irony that in</span>
  <span id="freeText2813236601521182663" style="display:none">What can one possible say of this novel? It is by far one of the most interesting pieces of American Literature of its time. One would want to consider it as science fiction or fantasy, all the while never feeling quite satisfied with either distinction (distinctions which are in themselves scrutinized in the story and possibly its most earnest (though disinterested) message). One thing for sure though is that with this novel Barth breaches that point of no return in meta-fictional irony that in my opinion has only ever been matched by David Foster Wallace (may he find peace at last).<br /><br />The plot of this novel is not far from his “Sot-Weed Factor,” the difference being that instead of creating a diaspora of interpretation, he uses cliché structures that fall in upon themselves, referencing each phase with the utmost conspicuous acknowledgement to its prior conventional use. To further his attack against distinction (aside from the obvious “pass all fail all) is his use of externalizing the book, making it something other than his own, and thus making the entire story itself irrelevant. <br /><br />As one would expect, you get all the snarky dank humor throughout the entirety of this novel. But much more is its significance in American Literature. It truly is a vision – one where the end both affirms and denies itself, highlighting the outrageousness of trying to find completion in our own lives through distinctions that never come but stifle, and ultimately making the always sought after Frankenstein of a book in which it takes on a life entirely its own: independent of author, editor, reader, and world.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="2813236601521182663" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_41868376" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/41868376" />
    <a title="terrycojones" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/550761-terrycojones"><img alt="terrycojones" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1212740225p2/550761.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/41868376?book_show_action=true">Jan 04, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="terrycojones" class="user" itemprop="url" name="terrycojones" href="/user/show/550761-terrycojones">terrycojones</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer41868376" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer653560537075263177">I read Giles Goat Boy in about 2000. I was 37 and I'd spent a lot of my life reading books. So you can imagine how infrequently I had the thought "this is the strangest book I've ever read". That's what came into my head several times during GGB.<br/><br/>It's also a masterpiece. An extended flight of fancy, totally bizarre, anticipating various aspects of our modern computational world. Best of all, it's a deadly anti-academic humor. It helps to have spent years in academia (particularly if academia drov</span>
  <span id="freeText653560537075263177" style="display:none">I read Giles Goat Boy in about 2000. I was 37 and I'd spent a lot of my life reading books. So you can imagine how infrequently I had the thought "this is the strangest book I've ever read". That's what came into my head several times during GGB.<br /><br />It's also a masterpiece. An extended flight of fancy, totally bizarre, anticipating various aspects of our modern computational world. Best of all, it's a deadly anti-academic humor. It helps to have spent years in academia (particularly if academia drove you nuts, as it did me) to really appreciate Barth's humor. I loved reading it. I'm sure I wont read it again, though in some ways I wish I could.<br /><br />Probably not the best book to start experiencing Barth. But wow, is it wacky, zany, ribald, and way way out there. Hats off to the kind of imagination that can produce something so different.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="653560537075263177" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_136640297" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/136640297" />
    <a title="David" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1439856-david"><img alt="David" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442653009p2/1439856.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/136640297?book_show_action=true">Dec 26, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="David" class="user" itemprop="url" name="David" href="/user/show/1439856-david">David</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer136640297" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6948179885898299962">I actually enjoyed this book more than "The Sot-Weed Factor." There is actually a similar theme running through the two books, though by no means is Barth rehashing old material and I did actually like this one better. The world of this book is just so interesting, a strange mix of the world as a university, Judeo-Christian material, the cold war, and others. Strangely approachable for Barth, this is probably my favorite book of his so far.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer295867069" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3756769037493430959">Just felt as though Barth was trying to include everything in this book, and as a result it felt messy and rushed. The whole idea of the two campuses as east and west, theology, politics, mythology etc etc. There aren't any characters to become engaged with, but I still found myself compelled to finish this.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('5ti/zr0CN2JCNze7NRox35bc2EG+lo/7XGUZJH7GOwt4sp+1REu6y2oeHNTvkAQisTdJMeAVy8bCZATM2+/vhg==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('ZkwLs7IWOuc90cIoGS+pbCD2UlcniEBG9LqL2NS+ylv4JivIS1+3ThX46UfDpZyRBx3DJ3kLBHtqu5YwcZce1g==')})

<script>

      var newTip = new Tip($('bookCover_11761'), "\n\n  <h2><a class=\"readable bookTitle\" href=\"https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/11761.Underworld?from_choice=false&amp;from_home_module=false\">Underworld<\/a><\/h2>\n\n      <div>\n        by <a class=\"authorName\" href=\"/author/show/233.Don_DeLillo?a=9&amp;origin=related_works\">Don DeLillo<\/a>\n      <\/div>\n\n          <div class=\"smallText uitext darkGreyText\">\n            <span class=\"minirating\"><span class=\"stars staticStars notranslate\"><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p6\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p0\"><\/span><\/span> 3.92 avg rating &mdash; 26,858 ratings<\/span>            &mdash; published 1997\n          <\/div>\n\n    <div class=\"addBookTipDescription\">\n      \n<span id=\"freeTextContainer7822120954562649181\">While Eisenstein documented the forces of totalitarianism and Stalinism upon the faces of the Russian peoples, DeLillo offers a stunning, at times overwhelming, document of the twin forces of the Cold War and American culture, compelling that \"swerve<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText7822120954562649181\" style=\"display:none\">While Eisenstein documented the forces of totalitarianism and Stalinism upon the faces of the Russian peoples, DeLillo offers a stunning, at times overwhelming, document of the twin forces of the Cold War and American culture, compelling that &quot;swerve from evenness&quot; in which he finds events and people both wondrous and horrifying.\n\nUnderworld opens with a breathlessly graceful prologue set during the final game of the Giants-Dodgers pennant race in 1951. Written in what DeLillo calls &quot;super-omniscience&quot; the sentences sweep from young Cotter Martin as he jumps the gate to the press box, soars over the radio waves, runs out to the diamond, slides in on a fast ball, pops into the stands where J. Edgar Hoover is sitting with a drunken Jackie Gleason and a splenetic Frank Sinatra, and learns of the Soviet Union\'s second detonation of a nuclear bomb. It\'s an absolutely thrilling literary moment. When Bobby Thomson hits Branca\'s pitch into the outstretched hand of Cotter—the &quot;shot heard around the world&quot;—and Jackie Gleason pukes on Sinatra\'s shoes, the events of the next few decades are set in motion, all threaded together by the baseball as it passes from hand to hand.\n\n&quot;It\'s all falling indelibly into the past,&quot; writes DeLillo, a past that he carefully recalls and reconstructs with acute grace. Jump from Giants Stadium to the Nevada desert in 1992, where Nick Shay, who now owns the baseball, reunites with the artist Kara Sax. They had been brief and unlikely lovers 40 years before, and it is largely through the events, spinoffs, and coincidental encounters of their pasts that DeLillo filters the Cold War experience. He believes that &quot;global events may alter how we live in the smallest ways,&quot; and as the book steps back in time to 1951, over the following 800-odd pages, we see just how those events alter lives. This reverse narrative allows the author to strip away the detritus of history and pop culture until we get to the story\'s pure elements: the bomb, the baseball, and the Bronx. In an epilogue as breathless and stunning as the prologue, DeLillo fast-forwards to a near future in which ruthless capitalism, the Internet, and a new, hushed faith have replaced the Cold War\'s blend of dread and euphoria.\n\nThrough fragments and interlaced stories—including those of highway killers, artists, celebrities, conspiracists, gangsters, nuns, and sundry others—DeLillo creates a fragile web of connected experience, a communal Zeitgeist that encompasses the messy whole of five decades of American life, wonderfully distilled.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"7822120954562649181\"       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/8113.John_Barth">About John Barth</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/8113.John_Barth"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1222685060p3/8113.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/8113.John_Barth">John Barth
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
536
followers
</div>

</div>
</div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer3093740350189166643">John Simmons Barth is an American novelist and short-story writer, known for the postmodernist and metafictive quality of his work.<br/><br/>John Barth was born in Cambridge, Maryland, and briefly studied "Elementary Theory and Advanced Orchestration" at Juilliard before attending Johns Hopkins University, receiving a B.A. in 1951 and an M.A. in 1952 (for which he wrote a thesis novel, The Shirt of Nessus).</span>
  <span id="freeText3093740350189166643" style="display:none">John Simmons Barth is an American novelist and short-story writer, known for the postmodernist and metafictive quality of his work.<br /><br />John Barth was born in Cambridge, Maryland, and briefly studied "Elementary Theory and Advanced Orchestration" at Juilliard before attending Johns Hopkins University, receiving a B.A. in 1951 and an M.A. in 1952 (for which he wrote a thesis novel, The Shirt of Nessus).<br /><br />He was a professor at Penn State University (1953-1965), SUNY Buffalo (1965-1973), Boston University (visiting professor, 1972-1973), and Johns Hopkins University (1973-1995) before he retired in 1995.<br /><br />Barth began his career with The Floating Opera and The End of the Road, two short novels that deal wittily with controversial topics, suicide and abortion respectively. They are straightforward tales; as Barth later remarked, they "didn't know they were novels."<br /><br />The Sot-Weed Factor, Barth's next novel, is an 800-page mock epic of the colonization of Maryland based on the life of an actual poet, Ebenezer Cooke, who wrote a poem of the same title. The Sot-Weed Factor is what Northrop Frye called an anatomy — a large, loosely structured work, with digressions, distractions, stories within stories, and lists (such as a lengthy exchange of insulting terms by two prostitutes). The fictional Ebenezer Cooke (repeatedly described as "poet and virgin") is a Candide-like innocent who sets out to write a heroic epic, becomes disillusioned and ends up writing a biting satire.<br /><br />Barth's next novel, Giles Goat-Boy, of comparable size, is a speculative fiction based on the conceit of the university as universe. A half-man, half-goat discovers his humanity and becomes a savior in a story presented as a computer tape given to Barth, who denies that it is his work. In the course of the novel Giles carries out all the tasks prescribed by Joseph Campbell in The Hero with a Thousand Faces. Barth kept a list of the tasks taped to his wall while he was writing the book.<br /><br />The short story collection Lost in the Funhouse and the novella collection Chimera are even more metafictional than their two predecessors, foregrounding the writing process and presenting achievements such as seven nested quotations. In LETTERS Barth and the characters of his first six books interact.<br /><br />While writing these books, Barth was also pondering and discussing the theoretical problems of fiction writing, most notably in an essay, "The Literature of Exhaustion" (first printed in the Atlantic, 1967), that was widely considered to be a statement of "the death of the novel" (compare with Roland Barthes's "The Death of the Author"). Barth has since insisted that he was merely making clear that a particular stage in history was passing, and pointing to possible directions from there. He later (1979) wrote a follow-up essay, "The Literature of Replenishment," to clarify the point.<br /><br />Barth's fiction continues to maintain a precarious balance between postmodern self-consciousness and wordplay on the one hand, and the sympathetic characterisation and "page-turning" plotting commonly associated with more traditional genres and subgenres of classic and contemporary storytelling.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="3093740350189166643" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
</div>
